[서술형]Step : The teacher organizes the students into two circles: an outerone and an inner one. Students in the inner circle face out,and those in the outer circle face in. The teacher then modelsthe dialogue, and the students practice it in pairs. The studentsin the inner circle take the role of Student A, and those in theouter circle take the role of Student B.Step 2 : After the students have practiced the dialogue with severalpartners, the teacher stops the activity and gives newinstructions. The students can refer to the dialogue but shouldnot read it. Instead, they look at their partner when they speak.The teacher continues to signal for the students to rotate.Step 3 : This time, the teacher tells the students to personalize thedialogue to make it their own. Some students will likely stickto the script, but others may choose to open up the dialogue.The goal is for students to have a real, natural conversation.: How are things going?: Not bad. How about you?: Pretty good, thanks. I’m a little sleepy, though.: How come? Did you go to bed late?: No, but I got up early. I didn’t want to be late today.: What time did you get up?: 5:30.: Wow, that is early. I got up at 7:45. I got here in plenty of저 2 nr Dr wpetime.: How did you get here? Did you drive?: No, I took a taxi.: Well, that explains it! I took the bus. I had to wait forever!ne Ww SP: Why don’t we share a cab tomorrow?412 Build-up Somg= 1In a fluency circle, the learners practice a dialogue in a fun andactive way. In this methodology, there are fivestages. In stages I, 11, and 111, the teacher focuses not only on thelanguage but also on being supportive of learners in their learningprocess. CBLT, as is seen in <A>, seeks tomake a focus on the outcomes of learning a central planning stage in thedevelopment of language programs. Traditionally, in language teachingplanners have focused to a large extent on the content of teaching or onthe process of teaching. Critics of this approach argue that this concernwith content or process focuses on the means of learning rather than itsends. CBLT shifts the focus to the ends of learning rather than the means.As a general educational and training approach, CBLT seeks to improveaccountability in teaching through linking instruction to measurableoutcomes and performance standards.Fill in each blanks in the above comments with suitable word(s).Eee 00000Your answer Gy(2)Chapter 02 영역별문제 417Part24. Read the following, and follow the directions. [7/2/24]! | (1) |: Students are given a problem and a set of information.They must arrive at a solution to the problem. There isgenerally a single resolution of the outcome.| (2) |: Students are given a problem for which there are anumber of possible outcomes and they must choose onethrough negotiation and discussion.| (3) |}: Learners engage in discussion and exchange of ideas.They do not need to reach agreement.B_ Task-based syllabus like <A> is the one that is organized around tasks. that students will complete in the target language. A task is an activityor goal that is carried out using language such as finding a solutionto a puzzle, reading a map and giving directions, or reading a set ofinstructions and assembling a toy.While carrying out these tasks, learners are said to receive comprehen-sible input and modified output, processes believed central to secondlanguage acquisition. A number of second language acquisition theoristshave proposed tasks as a basis for syllabus planning.The basic claims made for a task-based syllabus are:— Tasks are activities that drive the second language acquisition process.— Grammar teaching is not central with this approach because learnerswill acquire grammar as a by-product of carrying their tasks.- Tasks are motivating for learners and engage them in meaningfulcommunication.418 Buildup 영어교육론Guideline for Pre-service TeachersFill in the blank in <A> with suitable terms.[Your answer 블리(2)(3)25. With the advent of Communicative Language Teaching, the focusshifted to fluency rather than accuracy, encouraging an almost exclusiveemphasis on suprasegmentals. However, just as ESL teachers haveacknowledged that an emphasis on meaning and communicative intentalone will not suffice to achieve grammatical accuracy, pronunciation hasemerged from the segmental/suprasegmental debate to a more balancedview, which recognizes that a lack of _can be attributed toboth micro and macro features. In other words, it is no longer a questionof choosing between segmentals and suprasegmentals but of identifyingwhich features contribute most to lack of | | 300 which will bemost useful in the communicative situations in which our learners will needto function.Complete the current view of the pronunciation instruction in <B> by fillingthe blanks with the same ONE word.| Your answer |Chapter 02 영역별문제 _ 43106. [ 기입 6 형 ]Two recent related trends in language teaching are focus on form andconsciousness-raising. Focus on form refers to the practice of explicitlydrawing students’ attention to linguistic features within the context meaning-focused activities. In other words, | (1) | comes first, and a focus on| (2) | comes second. The advantage of this reorientation is that “thelearner’s attention is drawn precisely to a linguistic feature as necessitatedby a communicative demand.” Learners are therefore more likely to see therelationship between language form and communicative function.[Your answer 불안(2)22. Read the following and fill in each blank with ONE word. [7/2/21]As learners consciously learn and practice specific reading strategies,the strategy moves from conscious to unconscious; from | (1) ‘| to| (2) . For example, guessing the meaning of unknown vocabularyfrom context can be listed as both a strategy and a skill in reading texts.When a reader is first introduced to this concept and is practising howto use context to guess the meaning of unfamiliar vocabulary he orshe is using a strategy. The use of the strategy is conscious during thelearning and practice stages. As the ability to guess unfamiliar vocabularybecome automatic, the readers moves from using a conscious strategy tousing an unconscious skill. The use of skill takes place outside the directconsciousness of the reader.Your answer gy(2)Chapter 02 영역별문제 45125.452Le 0000 이이 000 02 이Read the following and answer the question. [서술형]| 1. [7/22]AIn addition to using the word sorry, there are other ways to soften |disagreement.1. You can add a few words such as “Wait a minute...” Gin informalEnglish) or “Well...” before disagreeing. Also, notice the vowel lengthin the following examples.Wait a minute. I don’t agree with that.Well, I’m not so sure about that.2. To make disagreement even softer, more careful, you can appear atfirst to agree before adding the word but. Here is an example;A: Both humans and chimps use tools.B : 1 agree, but I think humans’ tool use is more complex.3. You can also add a tag question. Here is an example:A : Both humans and chimps use tools.B:I understand what you’re saying, but humans’ tool use is muchmore complex, isn’t it?4. Often, your intonation alone—not your words—signals the listenerthat disagreement will follow. This is perhaps the softest form ofdisagreement.Notice the subtle differences in intonation (especially vowel length) inthese examples.Agreement : 1 agree1 agree with that.I agree with that.Disagreement : 1 agree,...I agree with that,...1 agree with that,...Chapter 02 영역별문제 461PartSe See aa ee ee)Rin caes en ee기출변형및영역별문제00 Sean Ee a 수어 Bh wi aoe one SU agskete hears oe ont EH 8 BRR EAR AQ IONE econ So SIAR 2 age APSE ar Abeta ARM ARNSSUOL HRThese examples of softening disagreement can be very useful innatural conversations, as well as in role-plays. Teacher A comments the problem with knowledge-based listeningactivities. Read his journal and complete his viewpoint by filling the blankwith THREE words. [ 기입형 ]<Teacher A’s Teaching Journal>I think while-listening activities should be things which most studentscan do. Accordingly, I bet, activities which depend mainly on previouslyheld knowledge are not suitable for classroom while-listening work. It isbecause the level of knowledge may vary considerably within the groupand thereby those with more knowledge will succeed while thosewith less knowledge, but perhaps with greater listening skills, will not.Another problem with knowledge-based activities is that a great deal oftime may be used in filling gaps in people’s knowledge, and this canmean that less time is spent on the actual listening work, and studentswho already ‘know’ may lose interest. As a result, it is better to useactivities where the solutions lie withinChapter 02 영역별문제 46335.464eee Oa ee ~UE,ee Cee me rene역별문제Ms. Park, the 2nd grade English teacher of a middle school, providesstudents with guided practice for effective reading comprehension. Read the following, and follow the directions. [7/2/33]Prepare some short model dialogues illustrating strong emotion, andprovide one small cushion for each pair or small group.Teaching Procedure1. Divide your students into pairs or small groups. Give each pair orgroup a short dialogue which illustrates an emotion such as hate,EE do se on ob gb carts im, ett eeanger, frustration, surprise, happiness, etc.2. Ask the students to perform the dialogue as realistically as possible.When they get to the words carrying maximum stress they shouldthrow the cushion at their partner(s), or in the air to express theemotion. These data-driven operations obviously require a sophisticated knowledge of thelanguage itself. From among all the perceived data, the reader selectsthe signals that make some sense, that cohere, that “mean.”Virtually all reading involves a risk—a guessing game, in Goodman’swords—because readers must, through a puzzle-solving process, infermeanings, decide what to retain and not to retain, and move on. The students listen to a recording of the dialogue or the teacher reads it out.2. They discuss in pairs what they can remember about it.3. They listen to the dialogue again and are asked to make a note of thekey words used.4. They work in pairs again, pool the words they wrote down andtogether write up their best version of the complete dialogue.5. They are given a written copy of the original dialogue to compare withtheir own.An activity like <A> is a useful one for a number of reasons. Firstly, thereis a focus on listening and speaking—students need to understand thedialogue they have heard in order to talk about it or make notes on it, andif they didn’t understand certain parts there is a chance that they can behelped towards understanding through discussion with a partner.Ideally, a task like this encourages between students.Because the students work in pairs throughout the exercise, they needto discuss and agree on the suitability of different language items to beincluded in their dialogue and this serves the purpose of both encouragingthem to use the language items in speech, and focusing clearly onmeaning as well as form.Finally, it is conducive to noticing. The process they have been throughwill make them open to acquiring the language items which they hadbeen unable to reproduce when writing the dialogue themselves.44.476Fill in the blank in <B> with ONE word. [7/23]An informal way of keeping track of progress over a period of time isby giving progress achievement tests such as short weekly tests basedon the topics, themes, or structures that have been covered during thatweek. The results of these tests can be used to give students feedbackon their progress in relation to the course objectives and to help themidentify any areas where they might need to do more work. Used in thisway, the tests have a | (1) function. Another important purposeof progress achievement tests is to motivate learners by allowingthem the opportunity to demonstrate what they have learned. For thisreason, they tend to be designed so that learners achieve high scores.In addition to tests, there are a variety of other non-test procedures thatcan be used to gather ongoing information on learners’ progress andachievement, including systematic observation, conferences between theteacher and the student and self-and peer assessment. One method thathas been shown to be particularly useful is the (2) . It containsa collection of student work selected by the student that demonstratestheir efforts, progress, or achievement over a period of time.Chapter 02 SHH 문제 «491Fill in the blanks with suitable terms to make them fit in context.[Your answer gy(2)07. Some ofthe discussion is focused on a story the students have recently read,“Atlanta,” by Betty Miles, 1995. The story is about a young girl whodecides that she wants to determine her own future rather than have itdetermined for her by her father.Students themselves are particularly pleased (and sometimessurprised) at the improvement they see in their work. By conferencingindividually with the teacher about their portfolios, they often cancome to their own conclusions about their progress, can see what theirstrengths are, and where they need to improve.Conferences must assume that the teacher plays the role of a facilitatorand a guide, not of an administrator, of a formal assessment. Next,in Item 2, the wrong alternatives, c and d did not work at all as a distractor sinceanyone in either group did not choose them.For teachers, this provides information that they can use in a course design. Asfor learners, it encourages self-directed and autonomous learning. Also, it promptslearners to reflect their speaking process (ability), resulting in developing theirmetacognitive skill (strategy).(1) diagnostic (2) portfolio(1) discrete-point items (2) decontextualizedThe above examples show two contrasting examples of feedback. Example 1provides limited information. It tells us how learners have performed on a test,relative to each other. If we assumes that five marks and over constitutes a pass, thenthis information distinguishes a pass and a fail. However, because the information islimited to scores, it is not likely to assist the teacher in precise planning for a class.Neither do results in this form provide a useful guide to the learner on what needsto be worked on specifically in order to perform better. Example 2 is different. Theteacher's written comments not only indicate the strengths and weakness of thestudents’ writing but they may also assist students in monitoring their own progressand identifying specific language areas to develop further. They may highlight forateacher priorities in terms of future teaching, or recycling of points already covered.The information could also be useful to parents.The purpose of Questionnaire 1 is for learners to reflect on what has been learnedover a period of time and express it as marks out of 10. Through this, they aredirectly involved in assessing their progress and able to take responsibility inmaking decisions about their own language learning development.This shows analytic scoring of compositions which helps call writers (learners)’attention to areas of needed improvement (washback) for future study.face validity(1) formative (2) washbackBuild-up 영어교육론 IGuideline for Pre-service Teachers 33) y—13. Among these, context is provided in the third test while in tests <A> and <B> eachtargeted item is decontextualized. In the case of test <C>, test-takers are requiredto choose items to make them fit in context (given sentences). Tests <A> and <B>,on the other hand, measure individual words by dictating or giving word definitionrespectively.Next, in terms of practicality and validity, Tests <A> and <B>, decontextualized,have high practicality from an aspect that they are usually easy to compile andmark. These tests, however, might not be valid if their purpose is to predict thelearner’s reading ability, which involves using context clues to help work out wordmeaning. To make them valid and contextualized, Test A should be required towrite down whole sentences, not individual words. Test B, also, should be changedto put the words into sentences. Test <C>, meanwhile, can be productive test byremoving its multiple choice answers.14. The problem of this test is that all distractors are not the same form of word (not inthe same lexical categories) as the correct answer. Accordingly, the other distractorsexcept A should be changed into words such as strong, energetic, and athleticwhich contrast with the old man’s weakened condition.15This listening task may simply become reading comprehension tasks. Students whoare asked to listen to a story with periodic deletions in the written version may notneed to listen at all, yet may still be able to respond with the appropriate word orphrase.16. According to the analysis criterion each book could be used for different learnersand language classroom goals. First off, in terms of communicative lessons, Book1 is more appropriate than any other books. Specifically, it is identified as moreresponse-oriented when students are engaged in turn-taking. Besides, it providesmore oral extended discourse as listening or reading (input) materials than anyother books. Moreover, in the case of the criterion of ‘expected output,’ Book 1generates more student language production than other books do.Next, for the criterion of ‘mental,’ Book 4 involves students in accelerating theircognitive processing with various mental activities. For examples, it activatesstudents’. background knowledge and leads them to build (create) text. morefrequently than other books do. Also, it requires students to apply language rulesthat the other books do not. Finally, for the visual learners who have sensitivity toshape, size and color, Book 2 is the best one because it contains inputs mostly ingraphic form(s). On the other hand, for the rhythmic learners, who have an abilityto recognize tonal patterns as well as sensitivity to rhythm, pitch, and melody,Book 3 is proper since sound and music motivate them.Chapter 02 영역별문제 = =505육부 (1997) 제 ? 차교육과정중학교영어과교 are 해설, 서울: 대한교과서교육무 (1997 ( 제 7 차 ) 외국어과 (1), 서울: 교육영숙외 5 인 (1999) 영어과 교육론, 서울: 한국문화사덕기 (1998) 영어 교육론, 서울: 고려대학교출판부| 홍수 (1999) 영어평가및멀티미디어 교육론, 서울: 한국문화사최연희외 2 인 (1998) 열린교육을위한중학교영어 / 수학수행평가의적용과효과에대한분석 연구, 서울: 이화여자대학교사범대학Anderson, Anne & Lynch, Tony (2001) Listening: OUPBetsy Parrish (2011) Teaching Adult ESL(Practical Introduction): McGrawHillBrown, H Douglas (2000) Principles of Language Learning and Teaching, Fourthranm wWoOiNEdition: Pearson EducationBrown, H Douglas (2001) Teaching by Principles, An Interactive Approach toLanguage Pedagogy, Second Edition: Pearson EducationBrown, H Douglas (2002) Strategies for Success, A Practical Guide to LearningEnglish: LongmanBrown, H.
